    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer7109136106039853522">Harpagon est un vieillard odieux et avare. Avare jusqu'au ridicule, puisqu'il fait comparaître en justice le chat d'un de ses voisins pour lui avoir mangé le reste d'un gigot ! Avare jusqu'au sordide aussi. Ne cherche-t-il pas à prêter à un taux usuraire l'argent qu'il refuse à son fils ? N'est-il pas prêt à vendre sa fille à qui offre de la prendre sans dot ? Quant à sa p</span>
  <span id="freeText7109136106039853522" style="display:none">Harpagon est un vieillard odieux et avare. Avare jusqu'au ridicule, puisqu'il fait comparaître en justice le chat d'un de ses voisins pour lui avoir mangé le reste d'un gigot ! Avare jusqu'au sordide aussi. Ne cherche-t-il pas à prêter à un taux usuraire l'argent qu'il refuse à son fils ? N'est-il pas prêt à vendre sa fille à qui offre de la prendre sans dot ? Quant à sa prétendue attirance pour Mariane, elle ne résiste pas à sa fascination pour l'or. Peut-être la plus célèbre des comédies de Molière, L'Avare s'inspire largement de l'Aulularia, une pièce de théâtre écrite au IIIe siècle avant J-C par le poète latin Plaute.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7109136106039853522" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>
</div>

<div id="review_1069844379" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1069844379" />
    <a title="Ahmad Sharabiani" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/614778-ahmad-sharabiani"><img alt="Ahmad Sharabiani" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1194930432p2/614778.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1069844379?book_show_action=true">Oct 02, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ahmad Sharabiani" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ahmad Sharabiani" href="/user/show/614778-ahmad-sharabiani">Ahmad Sharabiani</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/2856559">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=17th-century">17th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=plays">plays</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=french">french</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=european">european</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1069844379" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer49927789653839674">L'Avare = The Miser, Molière <br/>The Miser (French: L'Avare) is a five-act comedy in prose by the French playwright Molière. It was first performed on September 9, 1668, in the theatre of the Palais-Royal in Paris.<br/>The aging but vital Harpagon is hoarding every centime he can get his hands on, making sure that his two children, the virginal Elise and the dandy Cleante, live under his iron will. To complicate matters, Elise has fallen in love with the handsome Valere, who masquerades as a servant in t</span>
  <span id="freeText49927789653839674" style="display:none">L'Avare = The Miser, Molière <br />The Miser (French: L'Avare) is a five-act comedy in prose by the French playwright Molière. It was first performed on September 9, 1668, in the theatre of the Palais-Royal in Paris.<br />The aging but vital Harpagon is hoarding every centime he can get his hands on, making sure that his two children, the virginal Elise and the dandy Cleante, live under his iron will. To complicate matters, Elise has fallen in love with the handsome Valere, who masquerades as a servant in the household, despite his noble birth, and, worse yet, Cleante and Harpagon are both smitten with the same woman, the beautiful, if somewhat dim, Marianne. Meanwhile, scheming servants and assorted hustlers angle for Harpagon's incredible wealth, much of which is now buried and protected by snarling Dobermans. The delirious plot spirals to a wildly comic finish, filled with all the masterful plot twists and outrageous revelations one would expect from one of Molière's finest plays.<br /><br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: روز دوم ماه اکتبر سال 1972 میلادی<br />عنوان: خسیس؛ اثر: مولیر؛ مترجم: محمدعلی جمالزاده؛ مشخصات نشر: تهران، بنگاه ترجمه و نشر کتاب، 1336، در 171 ص؛ موضوع: نمایشنامه های نویسندگان فرانسوی - سده 17 م<br /><br />خسیس، عنوان مشهورترین نمایشنامه ی ژان باتیست پوکلن، متخلص به مولیر، درام‌نویس فرانسوی است. در خسیس، مولیر، طنز را چاشنی حرص و طمع زشت یک انسان (هارپاگون)، قرار می‌دهد، به طوری که در طول داستان، دیالوگ‌های هارپاگون، در عین اینکه خنده را با تماشاگر همراه می‌کند، نفرت خوانشگر را نیز به هارپاگون، بیشتر می‌نماید. اشخاصی مالدوست، که عشق خویش را، با پول تقسیم می‌کنند. قهرمان داستان، مرد ثروتمندی است بنام هارپاگون، که زن او مرده، پسری به نام: «کلئانت» و دختری به نام «الیز» دارد. پولهای هاپاگو،ن به جانش بسته‌ است، تا حدی که پول خود را در زیر خاک، پنهان می‌کند، و روزانه چند بار، به آن سر می‌زند، زیرا باور دارد، بهترین طعمه، برای جلب دزدها، صندوق‌های آهنی است، و نخستین چیزی هم که دزیده می‌شود، گاوصندوق است، همچنین دختر خود را، به مردی می‌دهد، که جهیزیه نخواهد. پسرش «کلئانت» عاشق دختری، به نام «ماریان» می‌شود، او حاضر است در صورت توافق نکردن پدرش، با ازدواج او با ماریان، به کشور دیگری سفر کند، و با پول کمی زندگی کند، ولی پیش از اینکه جریان ازدواج خود را، با پدرش در میان بگذارد، متوجه می‌شود، پدرش هم می‌خواهد، با ماریان ازدواج کند. «الیز» هم عاشق «والر» است، و برای اینکه پدرش «والر» را، به دامادی بپذیرد، «والر» در نقش مستخدم، وارد خانه ی هارپاگون می‌شود، تا نظر او را جلب کند. هشدار: اگر میخواهید نمایشنامه را، خود بخوانید، ادامه این نگاره پایان داستان را لو میدهد. «کلئانت» به همراه نوکر خود، تصمیم می‌گیرد، پولی که هارپاگون در باغ چال کرده بود را، بدزدد؛ سپس از پدرش میخواهد، با ازدواجش با «ماریان» موافقت کند، تا صندوقچه را به او برگرداند. از طرف دیگر مشخص می‌شود، «والر» و «ماریان»، خواهر و برادر هستند، و مردی که برای مسئله ی کاری، نزد هارپاگون آمده بود، پدرشان است؛ سپس خواجه «آنسلم (پدر ماریان و والر)»، تمام مخارج ازدواج را که هارپاگون بر عهده نگرفته، بر عهده می‌گیرد. در پایان «کلئانت» به «ماریان»، «الیز» به «والر» و «هارپاگون» به صندوقچه ی خود می‌رسند. ا. شربیانی</span>
  <a data-text-id="49927789653839674" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer4195834676688165110">Based on Plautus, this is one of five Moliere comedies I read in my rudimentary French (but good Latin) in grad school after one defeat or another. My paper at a post-doc at Harvard with Marge Garber compared Shakespeare and his Plautine sources. I've always considered the Bard best as a comedic writer, though one must concede Moliere his equal there.<br/> "L'Avare" is fairly late in Moliere's career, unlike Shakespeare's early uses of Plautus (here, "Aulularia"), and scandalously, in prose. A Duke d</span>
  <span id="freeText4195834676688165110" style="display:none">Based on Plautus, this is one of five Moliere comedies I read in my rudimentary French (but good Latin) in grad school after one defeat or another. My paper at a post-doc at Harvard with Marge Garber compared Shakespeare and his Plautine sources. I've always considered the Bard best as a comedic writer, though one must concede Moliere his equal there.<br /> "L'Avare" is fairly late in Moliere's career, unlike Shakespeare's early uses of Plautus (here, "Aulularia"), and scandalously, in prose. A Duke disapproved, but Boileau laughed, said to be the only one. Hence, earliest reviews dissed. In Aulularia, Lyconides confesses to a crime, "istuc facinus quod tuom sollicitat animum, id ego feci et fateor"(IV.x.734, Clarendon), exactly as in L'Avare, V.iii. <br /><br /> As for my defeats increasing my appreciation: My major defeat was a high-status, mentally failing prof who did NOT read grad student papers. Because a fellow student flattered him, quoted his Norton intro's back to him at his Ph.D. orals, the prof got him a fairly good job --at Princeton. That fellow wrote embarassingly poorly, so he made a better career... as college and university president.<br /> As a whole, Moliere's plays cheered me so much I didn't care what happened in the grad classes. I left--for years. Got my first job where 1400 people had interviewed for 3 positions: we were on national news, more applicants to teach than to go to college (1100?), Berkshire Community College, Pittsfield, MA in 1971. Was I better than the other 800 English apllicants? I was hired because of the way I pronounced "tromBONE." The Chairman had always heard, "TROMbone," but my Neapolitan music teacher (who played an Amati bass, and tuba) A. Bevivino, called it "tromBONE(AH)."</span>

    <a title="emily" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/58877035-emily"><img alt="emily" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1587215502p2/58877035.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2177896073?book_show_action=true">Nov 11, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="emily" class="user" itemprop="url" name="emily" href="/user/show/58877035-emily">emily</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780871294548" href="/book/show/257223.The_Miser">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2177896073" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11071329766872332369"><i>"What use will money be to us if it comes only when we are too old to enjoy it?"</i><br/><br/><b>This is my second encounter with Molière's brilliant work, and I, similarly to before, found it to be very entertaining.</b><br/><br/>Molière attention to <b>irony, exaggeration, humour, and also to darker matters</b> are what shape his comedies and make them truly great. <br/><br/>I strongly recommend <b>anyone</b>, who is only beginning to read plays and has struggle understanding and enjoying their structure, to read this one. <br/><br/>I laughed out loud at ti</span>
  <span id="freeText11071329766872332369" style="display:none"><i>"What use will money be to us if it comes only when we are too old to enjoy it?"</i><br /><br /><b>This is my second encounter with Molière's brilliant work, and I, similarly to before, found it to be very entertaining.</b><br /><br />Molière attention to <b>irony, exaggeration, humour, and also to darker matters</b> are what shape his comedies and make them truly great. <br /><br />I strongly recommend <b>anyone</b>, who is only beginning to read plays and has struggle understanding and enjoying their structure, to read this one. <br /><br />I laughed out loud at times, because of the witty dialogue and the hilarity of some particular events. The humor was fantastic, and this is coming from a person, who rarely ever laughs while reading. <br /><br />However, it also brought to mind many more serious themes, the most obvious one being miserliness, in other words, <b>greed</b> and the consequences of it. <br /><br />Whilst, at times the way it was presented was downright hilarious - there were moments when it really did create <b>a sense of disgust and weariness.</b><br /><br />An impressive insight into the lives of families at the time it was written, and <b>the paranoia, isolation and madness</b> such greed can cause.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11071329766872332369" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText7373495550014689335" style="display:none">This French classic was my first experience of Molière and made me a lifelong fan. Unfortunately, the translation in this Kindle edition (my copy is from Project Gutenberg) by Charles Heron Wall isn't as good as the one I remember from years ago (Richard Wilbur's?). While easy to read, I miss the rhyming couplets and the word play isn't as sparkling as I expect from Molière. <br /><br />Even with these flaws, I still had fun reading this. The ending reminded me of something from a Shakespeare comedy ("The Comedy of Errors" perhaps) but I love the fact that Harpagon stays miserly to the end.</span>

<div id="review_883311920" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/883311920" />
    <a title="Ben" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9592279-ben"><img alt="Ben" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1533140387p2/9592279.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/883311920?book_show_action=true">Mar 16, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ben" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ben" href="/user/show/9592279-ben">Ben</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9592279-ben?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer883311920" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16022412342290230581">How many of us have encountered someone very well off financially and yet cheaper than dirt? I've met my fair share of these people and it is with this type, the miser, that Molière is concerned in this work. I pictured the typical pop culture depiction of Dickens' Ebeneezer Scrooge when reading the story about the ever so miserly Harpagon, a man who cares more about his écus, which he never spends, than his family or anything else in the world, for that matter. Harpagon is undoubtedly a caricat</span>
  <span id="freeText16022412342290230581" style="display:none">How many of us have encountered someone very well off financially and yet cheaper than dirt? I've met my fair share of these people and it is with this type, the miser, that Molière is concerned in this work. I pictured the typical pop culture depiction of Dickens' Ebeneezer Scrooge when reading the story about the ever so miserly Harpagon, a man who cares more about his écus, which he never spends, than his family or anything else in the world, for that matter. Harpagon is undoubtedly a caricature, like so many characters sketched by Molière, and the conflicts that he encounters could not be any more hilarious, using many comedic elements that are still employed to this day (the servant with his hand held out for a tip when his master puts his hand in his pocket only to produce from it a handkerchief; double entendres galore; amplified suspicions, as when the police commissioner asks Harpagon whom he suspects of stealing his money and he replies, "Everyone"). There is nothing very sophisticated about this work, but it is extremely entertaining and is an interesting study in avarice with an ending that resolves in a way not dissimilar from many of Shakespeare's comedies or from some of Moliere's earlier works. Its great strength lies in its timelessness. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16022412342290230581" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/97396628-bruno?shelf=top-5-french-works">top-5-french-works</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/97396628-bruno?shelf=comedies">comedies</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2877219313" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12589448708914935597">After Plautus and Drzic, it's time to revisit a familiar plot about cheapness and resourcefulness, as well as tragic backstories. In this case, there's a significant improvement, giving the fact that there are four to five stories that intertwine within the plot and that some similarities with the source material are only in details (not just copied from page to page). There are far more characters and they are all unique in their own ways, whether it's a house servant, adviser, inspector or a h</span>
  <span id="freeText12589448708914935597" style="display:none">After Plautus and Drzic, it's time to revisit a familiar plot about cheapness and resourcefulness, as well as tragic backstories. In this case, there's a significant improvement, giving the fact that there are four to five stories that intertwine within the plot and that some similarities with the source material are only in details (not just copied from page to page). There are far more characters and they are all unique in their own ways, whether it's a house servant, adviser, inspector or a house owner. Not to mention the scenes, which are so comedic, that I've burst in laugh, even if they were serious (like appointing the task for servants). The love part between characters is OK, as each of them is predestined for someone, although in two cases there's love towards someone else, so the focus stays in the way how to avoid engagements, that they don't want. I read it twice and I don't feel bored at all, because there's always the need to look the other side stories, as I'm not able to concentrate on the names of each and every character during the first reading (not to mention the conversations that may drag to nowhere, but this one hits all the marks, so that I remember some of them). Definitely one of the best pieces from French literature.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12589448708914935597" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_754434697" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/754434697" />
    <a title="Maan Kawas" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17804085-maan-kawas"><img alt="Maan Kawas" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1586249006p2/17804085.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/754434697?book_show_action=true">Nov 01, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Maan Kawas" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Maan Kawas" href="/user/show/17804085-maan-kawas">Maan Kawas</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781162702445" href="/book/show/12321709-the-miser">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer754434697" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9348811849718455793">A very beautiful play, comedy of manners, by the great French playwright Moliere! The play is full of satire against the greedy avaricious misers, represented he by Harpagon, who can be considered as an obsessive greed. To Harpagon, money is more important than his children and their welfare, and he considers money his real treasure and he would not hesitate to accuse his son of stealing his wealth box and having him put into jail. The plot is so dramatic and beautifully constructed, and the eve</span>
  <span id="freeText9348811849718455793" style="display:none">A very beautiful play, comedy of manners, by the great French playwright Moliere! The play is full of satire against the greedy avaricious misers, represented he by Harpagon, who can be considered as an obsessive greed. To Harpagon, money is more important than his children and their welfare, and he considers money his real treasure and he would not hesitate to accuse his son of stealing his wealth box and having him put into jail. The plot is so dramatic and beautifully constructed, and the events move on so smoothly. In addition to the main theme, namely, avarice and its consequences on the miser and the people around, the other themes is about love, and the higher importance of love to money. Although the play is a comedy, still it shows how ugly it is to be a miser, and how such a quality negatively affect the life of the person and the person in his or her life. One of the points the play addressed was the rivalry between an old father and his son over a future bride, namely, Marianne, and the tension such a situation can generate. I could not leave the play until I had reached the final page.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9348811849718455793" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1770547683" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3313969224" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3313969224" />
    <a title="Richard Seltzer" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/110610415-richard-seltzer"><img alt="Richard Seltzer" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588366231p2/110610415.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3313969224?book_show_action=true">May 02, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Richard Seltzer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Richard Seltzer" href="/user/show/110610415-richard-seltzer">Richard Seltzer</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3313969224" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10370104742109865161">This article is based on a paper written in while a student at Brentwood School in Brentwood, Essex, England, May, 1965<br/><br/><br/>Act IV, Scene 7 (assignment = explication de texte)<br/><br/>It's quite easy to skip lightly over this passage, noting the irony of "j'ignore... qui je suis, et ce que je fais," the exaggerated images carried to ridiculous lengths, the sentence by sentence development and logic, his all-inclusive suspicion, etc. Then a few comments about Harpagon's distorted sense of values (poor chap), a</span>
  <span id="freeText10370104742109865161" style="display:none">This article is based on a paper written in while a student at Brentwood School in Brentwood, Essex, England, May, 1965<br /><br /><br />Act IV, Scene 7 (assignment = explication de texte)<br /><br />It's quite easy to skip lightly over this passage, noting the irony of "j'ignore... qui je suis, et ce que je fais," the exaggerated images carried to ridiculous lengths, the sentence by sentence development and logic, his all-inclusive suspicion, etc. Then a few comments about Harpagon's distorted sense of values (poor chap), about his love of money being like love of a woman, about his slapstick acting and talking directly to the audience, making it obviously comedy, about his separation from the world of the other characters being emphasized by his even noticing the audience...<br /><br />But this play has a deeper level on which it can be read more meaningfully.<br /><br />I asked myself:<br />Why should a man of his age<br />Be alone, alone<br />On a wide, wide stage<br />In a world of his own?<br /><br />A man can't be born an exile, un etranger. One also can't change over night.<br /><br />In this scene (IV 7) for the first and only time the depth Harpagon's feelings is immediately apparent. The object of his passionate, self-less, never-ending love is money; but money is an unnatural object for emotion: he could not have loved it thus from his birth. Here then is a highly sensitive man, who builds his whole world on the object of his love, who is totally lost, helpless, as good as dead without what is dearest to him. Consider this man as young; consider him as speaking about a woman, his dearly beloved, his wife for instance.<br /><br />The clue lies in this character who is never present, never mentioned, never alluded to: Harpagon's dead wife. The children obviously don't have very fresh memories of her, for Cleante in his anger would hardly refrain from including her memory in his reproaches of his father's actions. It is therefore natural to assume that she died shortly after the birth of the children and that Harpagon, who is so capable of surrendering himself completely in love, loved her deeply.<br /><br />Add to this wife the many other possible lost loves he could have had, loves upon whom he could likewise have centered, founded his world: mother, friend, pet... Think of Felicite in Flaubert's "Un Coeur Simple": one lost love after another, one resulting misery after another, coupled with a need to love someone, something. She finally transfers her love to the stuffed corpse of the parrot she had loved; she ends up, in sheer emotional self-defense or exhaustion, loving an object which is already dead and therefore cannot die, which she can safely trust she won't be deprived of.<br /><br />Jump back to Harpagon, similarly oppressed by "the slings and arrows of outrageous fortune" but not just un coeur simple, on the contrary highly intelligent and sensitive, with depths of feeling Felicite is incapable of: a tragic rather than a pathetic character (at this stage at least). Instead of a stuffed parrot, his defense, his shield, his wall (hence his "raideur") is in making money the object of his love. The soul may endure forever in another world; but in this one, money is more obviously permanent: it can be buried and continually watched, guarded to ensure it's continued presence.<br /><br />Thus the exile. Money has taken the place of man. THink of the passage by Donne about "no man is an island." Replace man with coin or a denomination of money, and the similar emotion of Harpagon is expressed: another's waste of money touches him personally.<br /><br />Think of the whole play with this in mind. The Mariane incident is a faltering step back toward normality. Harpagon lowers the stiff shield a moment and partially bares himself to possible pain. He considers marrying Mariane even if she doesn't have any money. No wonder he attacks his son so mercilessly with no holds barred (IV 3). No wonder he clings all the more tightly to his "chere cassette" in the last scene after he has lost this last human contact, last renaissant human love. Harpagon is in fact the only dynamic character in the play.<br /><br />Ah! Then shows forth the profound irony of the play. These "hommes raisonables", "hommes naturels" with their normal human relationships and love: they are revealed throughout and especially in I 1 and IV 1 as of many words, precious words, highly scrupulous about conventional forms, not too likely to get carried away by passion. Among them "...chacun tient les memes discours. Tous les homes sont sembables par les paroles; et ce n'est que les actions qui les decouvrent differents." These foolish wretches capable of only superficial feeling, these puppets, these pointless machines going through the actions of the fertility cults, doing just as expected, prescribed just at the right moment: they, irony of ironies, consider Harpagon as a feelingless automaton, a comic character incapable of human emotion. If they be basking in the light of reason and nature, then surely Harpagon is buried, buried in a deep dark pit of emotion, close to the heart of experience. The contrast is not between sane and insane, right and wrong, but between shallow and deep.<br /><br />Then what meaning this scene pours forth! "Je ne jette mes regards sur personne qui ne me donne des soupcons, et tout me semble mon voleur." Sure, on the surface, quite comic, quite ridiculously suspicious. But further on, Moliere makes clear his meaning. "Ils me regardent tous, et se mettent a rire. Vous verrez qu'ils ont part sans doute au vol que l'on m'a fait." And indeed they do take part in the theft: they -- all who associate with him -- laught at him, help rob him of his precious reason, push him further in his folly. No one cares for him or bothers to try to understand him. If they would, he would certainly be ressucite, could begin life anew. But they just humor him, use him, and laugh at him. He himself is also guilty; but his is a guilt of cowardice, a cowardice related to that of suicide rather than the callousness of the others.<br /><br />Reread the play and you'll see that practically every word takes on a new significance.<br /><br />So now you say, "Fine, you've rambled for pages. It's a curiosity, interesting enough for a moment's thought. But you are reading too much into the play, rather than studying the text itself. Moliere never thought of such things when he wrote it. He didn't intend such an interpretation." But this very question of intention takes the reader to the heart of the play, to the machinery that produced it, and with just one more step the controls are nearly within reach.<br /><br />If for a moment at least in IV 7, Moliere himself is speaking, when he speaks of guilt: personal guilt and the guilt of all who could laught at him, what could he mean? Isn't it the guilt, despair, frustration of a genius who realizes the extent of his power of self-expression but feels he has failed, prostituted it, sold it to the mob and thus failed to produce a work which he could consider worthy, of which he would dare to say: "This is my life, my masterpiece: whatever time or chance does to me or it: I created it; I completed it; c'est moi." Every time the audience laughed, didn't they push him further in his folly of writing only comedies fashioned to make the audience laugh more, of changing and suppressing passages and ideas to keep them laughing? And those laughs that kept pushing him and that he kept seeking in a vicious circle meant money.<br /><br />Does his life bear this out? His mother died when he was ten, and his first high-flying theatrical hopes crashed in debtor's prison; but such searching for miseries and evidence of love of money in the hope of making a point by point comparison between Moliere and Harpagon is futile and probably worthless. Two short quotes from the introduction should suffice as proof of the high probability of the essential: "Certain critics have regretted the fact that Moliere was obliged to expend so much of his time and genius on the writing of Court plays and ballets." "There is no doubt that he amassed a considerable fortune and was able to enjoy many comforts; he had much to make him happy but he was inclined to be morose: 'il fit rire, mais il ne riait pas.'"<br />Is it comedy or tragedy? Useless academic question. wAs life ever either purely comic or tragic? On rit et pleure tout a la fois. C'est un chef-d'oeuvre! C'est la vie! Final crowning irony: his despair at never having written his masterpiece produced his masterpiece: he did create himself in worlds; he actually placed himself naked on the stage and the crowd laughed at the emperor's new clothes.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10370104742109865161" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2684972652" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2684972652" />

<div id="review_1850856685" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1850856685" />
    <a title="Realini" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7873218-realini"><img alt="Realini" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1420269105p2/7873218.jpg" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7873218-realini?shelf=masterpiece">masterpiece</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1850856685" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8615005967384039254">The Miser by Moliere<br/><br/>Another version of this note and thoughts on other books are available at:<br/><br/>- <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://www.youtube.com/playlist?list=PLEVa4_CsRStSBBDo4uJWT8BSWtTTn0N1E'>https://www.youtube.com/playlist?list...</a><br/><br/><br/>This is at least the third time that I meet with Harpagon.<br/>Memorable is the film with Louis de Funes.<br/><br/>The last version is adapted for the BBC.<br/>I sympathize with Harpagon and I propose a different approach this time.<br/><br/>-	Yes, the old man is despicable from some points of view<br/>-	He fights with his children over arranged marriages, even competing with his son over the bet</span>
  <span id="freeText8615005967384039254" style="display:none">The Miser by Moliere<br /><br />Another version of this note and thoughts on other books are available at:<br /><br />- <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/playlist?list=PLEVa4_CsRStSBBDo4uJWT8BSWtTTn0N1E">https://www.youtube.com/playlist?list...</a><br /><br /><br />This is at least the third time that I meet with Harpagon.<br />Memorable is the film with Louis de Funes.<br /><br />The last version is adapted for the BBC.<br />I sympathize with Harpagon and I propose a different approach this time.<br /><br />-	Yes, the old man is despicable from some points of view<br />-	He fights with his children over arranged marriages, even competing with his son over the betrothal of Mariane<br /><br />Having established a couple of outlines, I am thinking that apart from the arranged, outrageous marriages there are problems with the marital relationships born out of love.<br />After a two years period, even the latter experience a honeymoon effect whereby a hedonic adaptation interferes.<br /><br />The partner can be as attractive and alluring as Brad Pitt or Angelina Jolie and still one wants a change.<br /><br />-	I would refer to the “Lasagna principle” as established and explained by the Harvard Professor Tal Ben Shahar<br /><br />Of course, clever people and especially those with a high EQ, which has been demonstrated to matter twice as much as IQ, would know how to avoid boredom and routine and half of marriages or more resist into old age.<br /><br />But I would point out that a couple that comes from the same group has a better chance to maintain the relationship than those who are very different- in spite of that claim that opposites attract- because after two years, if one is educated and preoccupied with the stories of John Cheever, while the other wants to talk about soccer, the gap will end up in becoming an abyss.<br /><br />Ergo, parents are sometimes right to insist on the viability of a marriage that does not bridge a gap as wide as that between a University professor and a plumber.<br />Of course, Harpagon is interested in a dowry for his son, who is to marry a rich widow and the opposite for his daughter, who is destined – if Harpagon would have the final say- to marry an old man, who would not ask for anything from the father.<br /><br />The parsimony of the protagonist is extreme and cause for ridicule and laughter, his community stigmatizing him.<br />When he has guests, he tells his servant to:<br /><br />-	Spare the wine, pour if they ask…and when they ask, wait, pretend not to hear…again and again<br />-	If they insist, add water…what about this apron, save it<br />-	How can I do that? It has a huge stain…and there is hole at my back, in the trousers…the people will see my…<br />-	Stay with the back at the wall<br /><br />Jacques is like one of those special offers that miserly fellows like: pay for just one and get…three in this case<br />For he is the cook, the servant and the coachman and he emphasis while asking his master- are you talking to the cook now?<br /><br />It is nearly hilarious at times, when Jacques for instance is appalled by the insincerity of Valere, who talks nicely about Harpagon:<br /><br />-	I know he is false master<br />-	How?<br />-	He says you are good, kind and a nice man<br />-	Well<br />-	We all know that is not true…<br /><br />Words to that effect<br />Then Jacques talks about the horses, that have nothing to eat in the house of The Miser, but they will collapse with this regime.<br /><br />-	After the horses, I like you most master and I don’t know why<br /><br />Harpagon is right with his leitmotif:<br /><br />-	We eat to live and not live to eat<br /><br />Studies made in Okinawa have demonstrated that the exceptional longevity of the people there is due in large part to their eating habits:<br /><br />-	They eat only about 80% of what they would like, they never get a “full stomach” <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8615005967384039254" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2400901622" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2400901622" />
    <a title="Mihailo Bozovic" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/82283076-mihailo-bozovic"><img alt="Mihailo Bozovic" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2400901622?book_show_action=true">May 23, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mihailo Bozovic" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mihailo Bozovic" href="/user/show/82283076-mihailo-bozovic">Mihailo Bozovic</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/8206261-the-miser">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">noone</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer2400901622&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2400901622" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7105000748348212581">I hate this book. It is one of the single dumbest dramas I've ever read. OK, let's start from the beginning. This book is about a cheap man named Harpagon who wants to marry the exact same girl that his son Cleante loves. Also there is the guy Valere in love with Elise and Harpagon wants Elise to marry this rich fella named Anselme. So, then Cleante steals some cash from Harpagon to marry Marianne. So, then there is a big argument between Cleante and Harpagon. In the end we have one of the most </span>
  <span id="freeText7105000748348212581" style="display:none">I hate this book. It is one of the single dumbest dramas I've ever read. OK, let's start from the beginning. This book is about a cheap man named Harpagon who wants to marry the exact same girl that his son Cleante loves. Also there is the guy Valere in love with Elise and Harpagon wants Elise to marry this rich fella named Anselme. So, then Cleante steals some cash from Harpagon to marry Marianne. So, then there is a big argument between Cleante and Harpagon. In the end we have one of the most contrived twists ever. So, this guy Anselme is actually Don Thomas d'Alburci and he had a shipwreck 16 years ago. His family fake died and then Marianne and Valere are his children. It comes out of nowhere and it makes zero sense. Why did this happen? How is this a natural turn of events? It's not explained at all. So, we've already established that the story sucks, well the characters are also bad. There is not a single likeable character in this book. Well maybe Frosine, but she was just like the most basic supporting character. Then, we have the writing. I think that this book was supposed to make people laugh, but all it did was making me cringe. All around, I can't possibly see why this book is so popular. Maybe it works better at a theatre or in its original language, but I don't know. I read it in my mother tongue which is Serbian so maybe that has something to do with the poor translation.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7105000748348212581" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/65191431-bernhardina-h-rnstein?shelf=feel-good">feel-good</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2312848707" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer609849070901401450">If you can make me laugh, you win me over. The Miser just did, with it's stereotypical, hysterical main character and the slightly confused bunch of people surrounding him. The play is about an old man named Harpagon who is greedy beyond words, although he has many to describe himself, his passion for money and all things of beauty. Everyone dislikes him, including his own children, for being rude and self-absorbed. Some of the most funny parts include the characters plotting against each other,</span>
  <span id="freeText609849070901401450" style="display:none">If you can make me laugh, you win me over. The Miser just did, with it's stereotypical, hysterical main character and the slightly confused bunch of people surrounding him. The play is about an old man named Harpagon who is greedy beyond words, although he has many to describe himself, his passion for money and all things of beauty. Everyone dislikes him, including his own children, for being rude and self-absorbed. Some of the most funny parts include the characters plotting against each other, being gullible or playing the game of life. Although it does have some strange turning points, especially by the end (I think it wraps up way too abruptly) "The Miser" is simple, straight forward and hilarious, as any comedy should be. Perhaps too straight forward - at times Moliére beats you over the head with his moralizing portrayal of greed - but its familiarity won me over from the start. Everyone is Harpagon, less or more, we all know him, and that is the point.</span>
  <a data-text-id="609849070901401450" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('NKfgnsSo0zwzrtbVs+v7tqHawB1t0k4BdCYKMNxi3W6/yQgYgeb6T9YK1vMEKkX4nWhiv2Eb0fljMXDK0z5TBA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('kb17YXfOEMWeb2F/DcXoG4DXh9mTo/6Xe2FR9Mj2yN4a05PnMoA5tnvLYVm6BFZVvGUle59qYW9sdisOx6pGtA==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

May, h<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText916322768523100211\" style=\"display:none\">Often compared to Byron, Keats, Shelley, and Poe, called Lautreamont\'s &quot;elder brother&quot; by the Czech Surrealists, Karel Hynek Mácha (1810-1836) was the greatest Czech Romantic poet, and arguably the most influential of any poet in the language. May, his epic masterpiece, was published in April 1836, just seven months before his death. Considered the &quot;pearl&quot; of Czech poetry, it is a tale of seduction, revenge, and patricide. A paean as well to nature, the beauty of its music and its innovative use of language, expertly captured in this new translation by Marcela Sulak, has ensured the poem\'s lasting popularity. Scorned at first by the national revivalists of the 19th century for being &quot;un-Czech,&quot; Mácha was held up as a &quot;national&quot; poet by later generations, a fate which the interwar Czech avant-garde, who considered him a precursor, took it upon themselves to reverse.\n\nUnlike the other seminal 19th-century European poets, Mácha\'s work has been largely ignored in English translation. The present volume, the only available in English, provides the original Czech text in parallel and includes a series of illustrations by Jindřich Štyrský specifically created for the poem.<\/span>\n       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/29837.Moli_re">About Molière</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/29837.Moli_re"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1219168412p3/29837.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/29837.Moli_re">Molière
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
1,088
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer15761997195204697278">Jean-Baptiste Poquelin, also known by his stage name, Molière, was a French playwright and actor who is considered one of the greatest masters of comedy in Western literature. Among Molière's best-known dramas are <i>Le Misanthrope, (The Misanthrope), L'Ecole des femmes (The School for Wives), Tartuffe ou l'Imposteur, (Tartuffe or the Hypocrite), L'Avare ou l'École du mensonge (The Miser), Le Malade </i></span>
  <span id="freeText15761997195204697278" style="display:none">Jean-Baptiste Poquelin, also known by his stage name, Molière, was a French playwright and actor who is considered one of the greatest masters of comedy in Western literature. Among Molière's best-known dramas are <i>Le Misanthrope, (The Misanthrope), L'Ecole des femmes (The School for Wives), Tartuffe ou l'Imposteur, (Tartuffe or the Hypocrite), L'Avare ou l'École du mensonge (The Miser), Le Malade imaginaire (The Imaginary Invalid)</i>, and <i>Le Bourgeois Gentilhomme (The Bourgeois Gentleman)</i>.<br /><br />From a prosperous family and having studied at the Jesuit Clermont College (now Lycée Louis-le-Grand), Molière was well suited to begin a life in the theatre. Thirteen years as an itinerant actor helped to polish his comic abilities while he also began writing, combining <i>Commedia dell'Arte</i> elements with the more refined French comedy.<br /><br />Through the patronage of a few aristocrats including the brother of Louis XIV, Molière procured a command performance before the King at the Louvre. Performing a classic play by <i>Pierre Corneille</i> and a farce of his own, <i>Le Docteur amoureux (The Doctor in Love)</i>, Molière was granted the use of Salle du Petit-Bourbon at the Louvre, a spacious room appointed for theatrical performances. Later, Molière was granted the use of the Palais-Royal. In both locations he found success among the Parisians with plays such as <i>Les Précieuses ridicules (The Affected Ladies), L'École des maris (The School for Husbands)</i> and <i>L'École des femmes (The School for Wives)</i>. This royal favour brought a royal pension to his troupe and the title "Troupe du Roi" (The King's Troupe). Molière continued as the official author of court entertainments.<br /><br />Though he received the adulation of the court and Parisians, Molière's satires attracted criticisms from moralists and the Church. <i>Tartuffe ou l'Imposteur (Tartuffe or the Hypocrite)</i> and its attack on religious hypocrisy roundly received condemnations from the Church while <i>Don Juan</i> was banned from performance. Molière's hard work in so many theatrical capacities began to take its toll on his health and, by 1667, he was forced to take a break from the stage. In 1673, during a production of his final play, <i>Le Malade imaginaire (The Imaginary Invalid)</i>, Molière, who suffered from pulmonary tuberculosis, was seized by a coughing fit and a haemorrhage while playing the hypochondriac Argan. He finished the performance but collapsed again soon after, and died a few hours later. In his time in Paris, Molière had completely reformed French comedy.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15761997195204697278" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
